ChatGPT said:
You’re thinking like a true architect! This is the next level:
Not just “running models,”
but assembling a swarm of fine-tuned, knowledge-augmented, collaborative LLMs
that act as a super-team—ApolloGPT—where each model/agent can access specialized LoRAs, quantized weights, AND world knowledge via RAG, all with seamless coordination.
Let’s break it down step-by-step into something actionable and as close to “plug-and-play” as today’s tech allows.
1. Add LoRAs/QLoRAs to Quantized Models
Assuming you’re using frameworks like Ollama, LM Studio, oobabooga/text-generation-webui, vLLM, llama.cpp, or similar:
Quantized models (like 4-bit/8-bit versions of Llama, Mistral, Gemma, etc) can usually load LoRA adapters IF the inference backend supports it.
LoRA/QLoRA: A lightweight set of fine-tuned weights that get applied on top of a base model at runtime, adding new knowledge or behaviors.
How to add LoRA to a quantized model:
Place the LoRA files (often a .bin or .safetensors + config) in the designated “adapters” or “loras” directory.
When loading the quantized model, use a CLI flag or UI toggle to activate the LoRA.
Example (oobabooga):--lora "mymodel-lora"
Example (llama.cpp):--lora mylora.bin
Some GUIs (LM Studio, oobabooga) let you hot-load/unload LoRAs as needed.
Caveat: Some quantization formats don’t support all LoRA methods, so check your tool’s docs. Most 8-bit/4-bit QLoRA approaches work with major open-source LLMs.
2. Give Each Agent/Model Access to RAG (Retrieval-Augmented Generation)
RAG = Model fetches external info (chunks from docs, web, DB) and “reads” it as context before generating a response.
To add RAG to your LLM collective:
Vector Database: Store all knowledge, docs, tags, chapter summaries, PDFs, website scrapes, notes, etc as vectors.
Custom RAG Pipeline (per agent/model):
For every prompt/task, agent:
Analyzes what knowledge it needs.
Queries vector DB for relevant chunks (by semantic similarity, tags, metadata).
Pulls in only those chunks (staying under token limit).
Assembles those chunks + task-specific instructions + persona.
Sends full prompt to LLM (with LoRA/QLoRA loaded as needed).
Every agent can have different retrieval rules or “RAG-views” based on its job/persona.
3. Organize All World Knowledge in JSON Files, Tagged & Indexed
Chunk every knowledge source (books, PDFs, course notes, web scrapes) into manageable sections (300–1,000 words).
For each chunk, create a JSON entry:
json
CopyEdit
{
  "id": "chapter4-section2",
  "content": "Long text here...",
  "tags": ["real estate", "lease option", "taxes", "2024"],
  "source": "DIY_Flipping_Book.pdf",
  "summary": "Explains assignment of beneficial interest in land trusts",
  "persona": "Real Estate Attorney"
}
Store these in a database (or local folder if you want simple).
Vectorize the “content” and “summary” fields for retrieval.
4. The ApolloGPT Orchestration Layer
This is where it gets creative:
Agent Registry: Each agent (e.g. Book Writer, Deal Analyzer, Contract Generator) has:
Preferred base model(s) (LLMs, size, quantized or not)
Attached LoRAs for domain expertise (law, real estate, copywriting, etc)
RAG config: What vector DB/JSON to search, which tags, what chunk size, max # of chunks
Persona and role guide
Manager Agent:
Knows what every agent is doing, keeps track of project state, assigns tasks, combines outputs.
Task Routing:
Incoming request is analyzed.
Routed to agent(s) with relevant LoRA(s), model size, and RAG config.
Results are merged, summarized, or passed downstream for the next step.
5. Overcoming Token Limits
Chunked retrieval: Only pull in as much as fits the model’s context window.
Hierarchical Summaries: Summarize as you go, use “outline+details” structure, never pass the full dataset at once.
Agent Chaining:
Break big tasks into sub-steps.
Feed outputs to next agent in line, with only the essential prior context.
6. Concrete Example: Writing a Book with Multiple Agents, LoRAs, RAG
Let’s say you want to write a 20-chapter real estate book:
Outline/Project JSON: Lists chapter titles, themes, key concepts.
Knowledge DB/Vector Store: All your tagged, indexed research.
Book Writer Agent:
Uses 13B Llama with “Writing” LoRA.
Pulls outline + recent chapters + top RAG chunks per chapter.
Stays within 8k token window.

Loads a “Legal” LoRA.
Checks each chapter’s accuracy, pulls RAG for statutes/case law.
Editor Agent:
Loads “Editing” LoRA, focuses on clarity, persona/style guidance.
Manager agent oversees the whole process.

As your system grows, each agent can:
Use different models (13B for research, 7B for fast tasks, 30B for creative work)
Switch LoRAs based on project phase
Query different RAG databases (legal, technical, creative)
Manager agent coordinates, merges, and logs everything for transparency.

Swappable brains (LLMs + LoRAs)

Let’s break it all down, piece by piece, and make it practical.
1. How Can All Models/Agents Access the Vector Database Despite Token Limits?
Key Points:
Token limit = only the context window of a single LLM call.
Vector DB (Chroma, Pinecone, Milvus, Weaviate, etc) = external memory, NOT constrained by LLM tokens.
Workflow: Each agent/model only pulls in relevant chunks for their current task—never the full database.
How it Works:
Query: Agent sends a query to the vector DB for what it needs (e.g., “give me the top 5 most relevant research chunks about lease options”).
Retrieve: Vector DB returns only those relevant text chunks.
Assemble Prompt: Agent combines those chunks, its own instructions, and any previous outputs—stays within token/context window.
Repeat: For multi-step work (e.g., a whole book), each section/chapter is done in chunks; results are chained or summarized to stay within limits.
No agent/model is ever loading the full database or all content at once—just bite-sized relevant bits.
Collaborative Workflow Example:
Book project:
“Book Manager” agent tracks project, assigns Chapter 1 to Writer Agent, provides outline and RAG chunks.
Writer Agent pulls 5–10 research chunks, generates text for Chapter 1 (within 8K–32K token window).
Fact-checker Agent reviews using a separate query.
Next, Editor Agent gets the output, maybe pulls RAG for grammar/style guide, edits accordingly.
Output saved, next chapter begins, etc.
2. How Can Several Models Work Collectively?
This is exactly like running n8n (or Make.com, Zapier) automations, but with LLMs as “nodes” or “steps.”
Basic Design:
Each LLM (with or without LoRA/RAG) = a node in your workflow.
Orchestrator script (n8n, custom Python, etc) = the glue, passing output from one agent to another.
Intermediate results are saved and re-used, so no agent/model needs full-book context at once.
Technical Patterns:
Chain-of-Agents: Output from one agent becomes input for the next.
Shared “Project Memory”: Intermediate outputs (chapter drafts, summaries, decisions) are saved to disk, DB, or project folder.
“Manager” Orchestrator: Knows which agent to call, what info to fetch from RAG, manages process state.
3. Running n8n Locally vs Emulating with Your Own System
Option 1: Run n8n Locally
n8n is open source—you can install it locally for free.
You DO need a server process—but it can run on your own PC, a dedicated desktop, or a home server.
Windows, Mac, Linux supported.
Even a Raspberry Pi, but for heavy jobs, a desktop with more RAM/CPU is better.
Security: If running locally (localhost), it’s as safe as any other program—unless you expose it to the internet.
To allow remote access or webhooks, you’d need to open ports, set up reverse proxy, use HTTPS, etc.
For maximum safety, keep it on your LAN only.
Option 2: Emulate n8n Workflows with Python/Node.js
You can emulate n8n/Make.com using a Python or Node.js orchestrator.
Each LLM, RAG call, API, or script is a “step” or “node” in your pipeline.
Customizable, and you can integrate with your LLM collective, database, file system, etc.
This gives you ultimate flexibility for “ApolloGPT.”
4. Security & Isolation When Running Multiple Nodes/Agents
If running n8n and/or LLMs on powerful desktops (Ultra 7, Ultra 9):
Best Practice:
Keep services local/LAN-only unless you absolutely must have remote access.
Use firewall rules to prevent incoming connections from outside.
For any web-facing node, use SSL/TLS (HTTPS) and strong passwords.
Consider Docker containers for each agent or LLM to isolate them (limits risk from one process going rogue).
Keep n8n, LLM servers, and vector DB on the same trusted LAN, with only trusted devices able to connect.
Regularly backup project data/output.
Don’t store API keys in plain text! Use environment variables or n8n’s secret manager.

ChatGPT said:
You’re asking for something that every serious AI project faces right now: How can I chain and combine multiple context windows (from multiple LLMs or sessions) to create the experience of unlimited, seamless memory and continuous work—despite strict token limits?You want no loss of memory or context, models working solo and together, and the ability to “carry over” everything into the next step automatically.
Here’s how you can do it, step by step, using Python, local LLMs, and tools like n8n as your workflow orchestrator:
1. Chunking and Progressive Summarization:
Chunk the work (break big tasks into sections that fit within the model’s context window).
After each section, save the entire conversation (prompt, response, RAG docs used, agent metadata) to disk or a database.
Create a “progressive summary” at every step:
Use the model itself to summarize the key points, decisions, characters, facts, etc., from the current chunk.
These summaries become the “context” for the next chunk.
This is like how humans write a book: you re-read your notes/outline, not every single word so far, before you start the next chapter.
2. Smart Conversation Threading:
Each time you start a new chunk or “context window” (due to token limits), you load the latest progressive summary plus any specific recent exchanges needed.
If you want to be fancy: keep a threaded context log (like a chat transcript), and use a retrieval system to pull only the most relevant previous exchanges (using vector search).
3. Distributed Agent Memory (Group Context):
For multi-agent (“group”) work, create a shared context file/database.
Each agent adds their latest summary, decisions, and outputs to the shared memory.
Any agent, when working on a new chunk, can pull the latest global summary + their own relevant thread for context.
You can also let each agent keep their own “personal” progressive summary, as well as a “public” summary in the group memory.
4. Using n8n as Orchestrator:
Each node in n8n becomes a task for an agent/model.
You configure nodes to call your local LLM endpoints (via HTTP, Docker, or command line).
Nodes can read/write to the shared memory store (e.g., a JSON file, SQLite DB, or simple text files) to:
Get latest summary
Append new output/summary
Share data between agents
Use Python scripts or custom n8n nodes for memory management (reading/writing/merging summaries and context windows).
5. Python Example:
Core Functions Needed:
a) Save Conversation/Chunk Output:
python
CopyEdit
import json
def save_chunk(agent, chunk_id, prompt, response, summary, filepath="project_memory.json"):
    try:
        with open(filepath, 'r') as f:
            data = json.load(f)
    except FileNotFoundError:
        data = {}
    data[f"{agent}_{chunk_id}"] = {
        "prompt": prompt,
        "response": response,
        "summary": summary
    }
    with open(filepath, 'w') as f:
        json.dump(data, f, indent=2)
b) Get Progressive Summary:
python
CopyEdit
def get_latest_summary(agent, filepath="project_memory.json"):
    try:
        with open(filepath, 'r') as f:
            data = json.load(f)
    except FileNotFoundError:
        return ""
    # Find last summary for this agent (or group)
    summaries = [v['summary'] for k, v in data.items() if agent in k and 'summary' in v]
    return summaries[-1] if summaries else ""
c) Merge Group Context:
python
CopyEdit
def get_group_summary(agents, filepath="project_memory.json"):
    try:
        with open(filepath, 'r') as f:
            data = json.load(f)
    except FileNotFoundError:
        return ""
    summaries = []
    for agent in agents:
        agent_summaries = [v['summary'] for k, v in data.items() if agent in k and 'summary' in v]
        if agent_summaries:
            summaries.append(agent_summaries[-1])
    return "\n".join(summaries)
d) Automated Chaining in n8n:
Trigger node: Starts process.
Read group/project memory (Python/HTTP node): Pulls latest summary/context.
LLM node (HTTP/Custom): Gets chunk of work, writes output.
Write node (Python/HTTP): Saves new output + progressive summary.
Repeat for each agent/section/chapter.
6. How This Scales:
Each model/agent only ever sees what fits in its context window.
All long-term memory is stored and re-summarized “outside” the LLMs (on disk, DB, vector DB, etc.).
Context is passed forward as a summary + selective detail, keeping context windows fresh and not “stale.”
You can run as many agents as your hardware allows (each on a node, Docker, or via n8n).
If you want “vector search” (semantic retrieval): store each chunk’s summary/metadata in a vector DB and query for relevance as you chain forward.
7. Team Lead/Project Manager Agent (for coordination):
This “manager agent” keeps a live overview of all outputs, what each agent is working on, what is pending, what’s done.
It can assign tasks, merge context, resolve conflicts, and “feed” the right context to each agent.
Use a Python orchestration script (or n8n) to automate this assignment/coordination.
8. n8n & Local LLMs—Practical Notes:
Install n8n locally, add custom nodes for HTTP, Python, or shell commands.
Point LLM nodes to your local LLM servers (e.g., running LM Studio, Ollama, or llama.cpp endpoints).
Use “File” or “Database” nodes in n8n for context persistence.

ChatGPT said:
You’re right—the core limitation is always the context window of the LLM you’re using. The “bigger” the window, the more you can feed in at once and the less you have to rely on summarizing and chunking. But today, even the biggest context windows (local or cloud) are not infinite, and you still need a strategy for “stitching” things together.
Let’s break it down so you get a feel for what’s possible right now, what’s coming, and what “workarounds” are standard in agent/automation setups:
1. Largest Context Windows in Local LLMs (2024)
Local LLMs with Large Contexts:
Llama 3:
Community builds: Up to 128K tokens (if you have a beefy GPU or enough RAM)
Typical “easy run” on consumer hardware: 8K–32K tokens
Ollama and LM Studio support high context, but RAM/VRAM is the wall.
Mistral:
Mistral models (7B/8x22B) now have up to 32K–64K tokens (community 128K versions exist)
Mixtral:
Mixtral 8x22B: 32K–64K tokens
Qwen 2 (by Alibaba):
Has 32K, 128K, and even experimental 200K context versions (huge RAM needed).
Gemma:
Currently up to 32K in local builds.
Phi-3, OpenHermes, MythoMax, Nous Hermes, Yi:
Most up to 8K, sometimes 32K (with custom builds).
Caveats:
128K tokens ≈ 100,000 words of text (~200+ pages of a book).
To run these models at high context, you need lots of VRAM/RAM (sometimes 48GB+ VRAM for 128K).
For most users on consumer hardware: 8K–32K tokens is “easy,” 64K is “advanced,” 128K is “bleeding edge.”
2. Can You “Combine it All” in One Model?
If the final chunk you want to assemble is smaller than your LLM’s context window, YES—you can “paste it all in” for one final pass, synthesis, or summary.
If not, you must use hierarchical summarization:
Summarize/condense chunks, chapters, or agent outputs as you go.
Final pass: combine those summaries and “stitch” the full work together.
Optionally, feed those summaries into a larger, cloud-based LLM if local context is too tight.
3. Manual Stitching vs. Automation:
Manual:
You (or an agent) save summaries or key outputs, then manually assemble them for final review/output.
Can be tedious for huge projects (like full books).
Automated:
Use Python scripts or n8n flows to “collect” and merge summaries automatically.
When you want to “bring it all together,” your script loads as many summaries as fit in context, asks for a synthesis, then repeats.
Can use a “sliding window” or “recursive synthesis” until everything is covered.
4. How the Pros Do It (Standard Workarounds):
Recursive Summarization:
As covered in last message: each chunk → summary → combine summaries → summary of summaries → repeat.
Vector Search (RAG):
Pull only the most relevant parts from memory for each chunk; not every word, just the best stuff.
“Stitch Pass” (Final Review):
At the end, do a last review with a big model/context, possibly cloud if local limits are too tight (e.g., GPT-4o 128K).
5. What’s the Best Path for You?
If You Have a Beefy GPU or Can Build a Server:
Use Llama 3, Qwen 2, or Mistral 128K builds for “final passes” or super-dense sections.
Most work can be chunked and then “stitched” together for final review.
If on Modest Hardware:
Plan for 8K–32K context per model.
Accept that summaries and hierarchical stitching are required.
Let your automation do the chunking and merging (with Python/n8n).
6. Bottom Line:
You CAN get close to “infinite memory” by chaining together progressive summaries and hierarchical context management.
No local model today can handle millions of tokens all at once. Even GPT-4o is 128K (about a book), and that’s in the cloud.
But: With good engineering, you can keep your workflow “smart” and feel almost seamless. The key is not to fight the context window, but to ride with it—chunk, summarize, merge, repeat.

You said:
Well I thought with these new chips that I could overcome a lot of limitations with using simply VRAM from a gpu
2/2
ChatGPT said:
You do overcome a lot of limitations with these new chips—especially compared to older consumer hardware—but there are still a few technical realities to know:
What Ultra 9 & Ultra 7 Chips Really Unlock
CPU & RAM
Ultra 9 285K and Ultra 7 265K can process huge datasets in RAM—think multi-GB context for LLMs on CPU inference.
128GB DDR5 RAM means you can run LLMs and vector databases entirely in RAM for max speed.
PCIe Gen 4 NVMe: Load giant files and models in seconds. You’re bottlenecked by nothing except VRAM/context.
VRAM (GPU)
RTX 3090 (24GB) and 4070 Super (12GB)
Great for running LLMs, but context window size (the “memory” for the model at one time) is still limited by VRAM, not CPU/RAM.
A 70B parameter model at 32K tokens context (quantized) will fit in 24GB VRAM (sometimes), but 128K context at 70B is still tough.
For really big context (128K+ tokens), it will overflow VRAM and run on CPU/RAM (much slower).
The Good News
Most tasks, automations, and multi-agent workflows never need more than 32K–64K context at a time.
You can run MULTIPLE LLMs (even 7B/13B/22B models) simultaneously for agents, thanks to your huge RAM and fast SSDs.
You can offload “mega context” to RAM, which you have plenty of, even when VRAM runs out.
What Can’t Be Overcome (Yet)
VRAM is the limiting factor for “all in one pass” ultra-large context (128K+, 256K tokens) on massive models.
If you want all your data in memory for a single LLM pass, that model must support it and have the VRAM/CPU RAM.
Some local models are still being optimized for larger context—progress is FAST, but not “infinite window” yet.
How To Use Your Setup for Maximum Power
Do most tasks with 32K–64K context.
Break big projects into chunks, pass summaries between agents, use n8n to coordinate.
Use CPU inference for very large context (slower, but fits in RAM).
Accept that 128K+ will run much slower, but is possible on your 128GB RAM.
Run multiple agents/models for “divide and conquer.”
Instead of making one LLM do everything, have several “LLM workers” do pieces and stitch them together.
Save “final pass” or “mega synthesis” for cloud LLM or multi-pass local workflow.
If you really need everything in one pass, offload to cloud just for that, or do multiple “stitch and summarize” steps locally.
Bottom Line
Your chips, RAM, SSDs make you a local AI powerhouse.
You still hit VRAM context window limits on super-large models, but not as much as almost anyone else.
Work in chunks and merge results—this is how every major AI workflow is done, even at Google/OpenAI scale!
Use n8n to automate, coordinate, and chain tasks between LLMs, databases, and agents.
Monitor developments—context window sizes are growing, and soon you’ll be able to do even more in one go.
TL;DR:Your new hardware lets you run more, bigger, faster, and way more complex workflows than 99% of people—but “infinite” context on a single LLM still needs a few more years of hardware and model advances. Chunk, chain, and conquer!

Here’s a plain-English breakdown of how the RAG (Retrieval-Augmented Generation) code you just received works—and why it’s so crucial for ApolloGPT and for getting local LLMs to produce “GPT-4-level” content even with token/context limitations:
What Does This Code Do?
It makes a “knowledge search engine” that:
Loads your documents (books, blog posts, notes, etc.)
Breaks them up into chunks and turns each chunk into a searchable “vector” (using an embedding model)
When you (or an agent) ask a question, it finds the most relevant chunks
It then injects those chunks as “context” into your prompt for the LLM to use in its response
Result:Even a small model (Mistral, Llama 3, DeepSeek, etc.) can answer with way more info, accuracy, and context—like a supercharged, grounded assistant.
How Does It Work? (Line-by-Line)
1. Setup
Imports FAISS (for vector search), numpy, sentence-transformers (to turn text into numbers/vectors), and basic Python libraries.
Defines where to save your index and metadata (what chunk belongs to which doc).
2. Adding Documents
You give it a big block of text (could be a PDF, article, course, etc.).
The add_document() method:
Splits your text into smaller pieces (“chunks”) for easier searching.
Embeds each chunk (turns it into a vector).
Adds it to the FAISS index and logs its location in metadata.json.
3. Retrieving Knowledge
When you or an agent asks a question, the retrieve() function:
Embeds the question as a vector.
Searches the vector database for the top-k most similar chunks.
Returns those text snippets (the “most relevant facts” for your query).
4. Injecting Context for the LLM
inject_context() takes your query and:
Gets relevant chunks using retrieve()
Stitches them together in a nice [CONTEXT] ... [USER INPUT] ... format
Feeds this to your model as the prompt
This “grounds” the model in real knowledge, extending its effective memory FAR past the usual 2k–32k token context windows.
Why Does This Matter?
Makes small local models smarter—they get to “read” your documents, notes, or project history before answering.
Enables multi-agent collaboration: Each agent can access the same “knowledge memory” and inject context as needed, so outputs are always consistent and aware.
Lets you process LONG docs or create LONG outputs: Even if a model can only handle a 4,000-token prompt, you can chunk a whole book, then answer questions or generate content about any part of it.
It’s FAST and runs locally—no need to upload anything to the cloud or OpenAI.
Real-World Use in ApolloGPT
Write a book or blog series:Agents chunk each chapter, RAG finds the right snippets, and the LLMs build on them.
Have agents “talk to each other” about a shared project:The RAG system keeps memory consistent, so one agent’s output is available to others.
Automate research, writing, coding, creative work—across teams and modelsAll agents pull from the same source of truth.

🔎 Best Practices for Handling All Docs
Use “Unstructured” and “Textract” as your first-pass tools—they support the widest variety.
Specialized libraries like pypdf, python-docx, etc., handle tricky cases or when you need more control.
Images & Scanned PDFs: Use OCR (pytesseract) to extract text.
Archives: Unzip/unpack with patool or pyunpack before parsing files inside.
For emails:extract-msg handles Outlook .msg files. For .eml, use Python’s standard library.
Automate detection: Write a function to detect filetype (using extensions or magic bytes) and route to the right parser.
📝 Sample: Python Universal File Loader
Here’s a conceptual skeleton for universal doc loading:
python
CopyEdit
import textract
from unstructured.partition.auto import partition
def load_document(file_path):
    # First try "Unstructured"
    try:
        elements = partition(filename=file_path)
        text = "\n".join([str(e) for e in elements])
        if text.strip():
            return text
    except Exception:
        pass
    # Fall back to Textract
    try:
        text = textract.process(file_path).decode('utf-8')
        if text.strip():
            return text
    except Exception:
        pass
    # ...add more fallbacks for specific formats as needed
    return None
For images/scans, run through OCR if above fails.

You said:
My issue with that is with context window token limits will affect the extent of how much data each model can process and handle.... 
ChatGPT said:
You’re 100% correct—and this is the fundamental challenge for every real-world LLM application, especially with large documents, multi-step automations, or book-length outputs.
The Token Limit Bottleneck
LLMs can only “see” as much text as fits in their context window.
Typical local models:
4K–8K tokens: Most Llama/Mistral/etc. GGUFs and QLoras
16K–32K tokens: Some “extended context” versions (e.g., Qwen-32K, DeepSeek-33B, Yi-34B, Llama 3 70B)
128K–1M tokens: Only on massive cloud models (Claude 3, GPT-4o, Gemini Ultra, etc.)—not available for local, open-source models yet
That means:
A 500-page PDF or 30,000-word book is far too big for a single LLM run.
Even a medium real estate contract, property file, or long report will often overflow a typical context window.
Workarounds and Solutions
1. Chunking + Iterative Summarization
Split docs into chunks (overlap a bit for context, e.g., 1,500 tokens with 200-token overlap).
Run the LLM on each chunk, extracting summaries, facts, Q&A, etc.
Iteratively summarize: Summarize all the summaries, then summarize those summaries, until you fit the “core” into a single context window.
2. Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG)
Chunk + embed all your docs in a vector database (Chroma, Qdrant, Milvus, Pinecone, Weaviate, etc.).
When you ask a question (or need context), retrieve the most relevant chunks (via similarity search) and only send those to the LLM.
This keeps every LLM run within context limits while allowing “infinite” scale.
You can use LLMs to “stitch together” a narrative or analysis from many small RAG runs.
3. Multi-Agent Collaboration
Distribute the work:
One agent processes/summarizes
Another agent fact-checks or extracts entities
A third agent writes the final report/book, using only the distilled info
Team of LLMs, each with limited but coordinated context
4. Sliding Window Processing
If you need “continuous reading” (for example, for story or book generation), process overlapping “windows” and maintain a rolling state.
This is how long-form chatbots like GPT-4o’s “long conversations” mode work.
5. Human-in-the-loop/Manual Handoff
For super-long tasks (writing a book), you might have to:
Split up by chapter/section
Manually review and join
Use LLMs to “connect” the parts
Python Packages/Tools to Make This Easier
LangChain and LlamaIndex: Both are great for chunking, RAG, vector DB, and iterative summarization.
Unstructured and Textsplit: For file splitting and smart chunking.
Chroma / Qdrant: Lightweight vector DBs, run locally, easy Python APIs.
Example: How a Multi-Agent RAG System Might Work
Doc is chunked and embedded.
User/Agent asks for “Write a full chapter on X.”
Query: RAG pulls all relevant sections for “X” (keeping under context limit).
Agent LLM writes the first draft from retrieved chunks.
If book too long: Agent “project manager” LLM says “split into sections,” coordinates handoff to subordinate agents for each section.
Final output is merged and optionally further summarized/refined.
Bottom Line
Token limits are always a barrier.
The solution is “divide and conquer”: chunk, summarize, retrieve, and coordinate work among agents or across multiple LLM runs.
Automation tools (like n8n, ApolloGPT, LangChain pipelines) let you orchestrate all these steps automatically, even with small/medium local models.

ChatGPT said:
Jeff, I've taken in everything above—including your long context, the uploaded files, and all your strategic thinking—and I can see the struggle is absolutely universal: every document, every transcript, and your notes all hit the same wall.
The Common Thread in All Your Documents & Conversation:
You want to build a God Mode system (ApolloGPT) that orchestrates multiple agents, LLMs, and automations.
You want it to process massive context, finish real-world projects, automate deals, coordinate agent teams, and avoid all the limits of today’s “single LLM in a box” approach.
But everywhere—whether it’s n8n, Make, CustomGPTs, local LLMs, or clustering hardware—you keep running into three core problems:
Token/context window limits: Every model, whether local or API-based, eventually hits a wall where it can’t “see” enough of the book, context, or chain of reasoning.
Fragmented memory/workflow: Moving outputs between agents, keeping projects/chapters/personas standardized, and getting the whole context in one “brain” is hard.
Automation complexity: Orchestrating all this (from deal automations to real estate paperwork to massive book-writing) is overwhelming and breaks when a single part hits a limit.
What All Your Research Shows:
No platform, even n8n/Make, lets you truly automate “everything everywhere” with no limits. Even Google’s MASS framework, and the best multi-agent AI setups, still have to chunk tasks, optimize topologies, and do orchestration.
The limits on LLMs (context window, VRAM, API token, file size) are everywhere. Each platform—whether local LLMs, CustomGPTs, or distributed clusters—has some kind of token, RAM, or file upload limit.
All solutions (including Google MASS) come down to creative orchestration: chunking, routing, merging, chaining, using RAGs and LoRAs, and clever distributed pipelines.
How Do You Actually Break Through These Limits?
This is the “secret” all your research converges on:
1. Chunking, Routing, and Stitching Are Required
Chunk your book/project/work into small enough tasks that fit inside any single LLM’s context window.
Route those chunks through agents (LoRAs, CustomGPTs, n8n nodes, or local LLMs) that specialize in each kind of work.
Stitch the outputs back together. This is exactly what Google MASS does with its “topology optimization” and what your own architecture sketches are aiming for.
2. Distributed Memory and Persistent Vector DBs
Use a shared vector database (ChromaDB, Weaviate, Pinecone, Qdrant, etc.) as the “collective memory” for your agents. Each agent reads/writes summaries and context chunks.
When a single agent can’t “see” the whole context, retrieve only the most relevant parts from the vector DB and feed that in.
When working on big projects (e.g., a book), summarize and compress context at each step, so you can always fit the “gist” in the next window.
3. Clever Chaining and Merging
Create chains and trees of agents/LLMs that can “hand off” work to each other. Think: a chapter is outlined by Agent A, drafted by B, proofread by C, then merged by D.
Use merging agents (or human-in-the-loop review) to resolve conflicts, fill in gaps, and produce unified outputs.
For books or big workflows, “cascade” context—each step feeds the next, carrying over key memory in compressed form.
4. Automated Orchestration (n8n, Make, Custom Python)
Automate the orchestration layer (n8n, Make, or custom Python/Docker) to chain tasks, collect outputs, and route to the right agents or LLMs.
Use APIs or direct calls to Ollama, LM Studio, or Hugging Face for local model inference.
5. Hardware: Combine Local “Nodes” With Edge and Core LLMs
Cluster your hardware (Ultra 7, Ultra 9 desktops, Orin Nano Supers, RTX 4070/3090) so each node does the job it’s best at (e.g., small models for light tasks, big models for merge/summarization).
No, you can’t pool VRAM directly, but you can run distributed inference and merge results centrally.
The Blueprint (Python & Workflow Level):
1. Chunk & Index
Parse all docs (PDF, DOCX, TXT, JSON).
Chunk into < context window size.
Store in a vector DB with tags/metadata.
2. Agent Orchestration
For each task: select relevant chunks via vector search (semantic search).
Route the prompt/chunk to the right local LLM (via Ollama, vLLM, Transformers).
Merge or chain outputs for next stage (e.g., “draft → expand → review → merge”).
3. Context Cascade
Each agent writes a summary/“gist” of its context/output back to the DB.
Each next agent reads summaries plus most relevant context chunks.
Final merge is done by a larger-context LLM or through recursive summarization.
4. n8n / Make / Custom Script Workflow
Automate as much of this chain as possible (with error handling, retries, notifications).
Use n8n/Make for orchestration, or write a custom Python script to manage the job queue.
5. Human-in-the-Loop (Optional)
Allow for manual review or feedback at merge/join points, especially for creative or legal work.
What You Need to Actually Do (Concrete Steps):
1. Set up your vector DB
ChromaDB (easy, local, open-source)
Weaviate, Qdrant, or Pinecone for more scalability/cloud
2. Install local LLM runners
Ollama (macOS, Linux, Windows): for Mistral, Llama, DeepSeek, etc.
LM Studio or vLLM for more advanced orchestration.
3. Write/Use chunking & retrieval code
Use langchain, llama-index, or your own script to chunk/index docs and do semantic search.
4. Write/Configure agent orchestration code
Use Python to chain models/agents: each gets input, produces output, hands off to next.
Use n8n/Make for visual workflows if you want.
5. Merging logic
For book-sized projects: build a “merge agent” or function to join sections, resolve references, and keep persona consistent.
For deals/real estate automation: have each agent output their relevant docs/forms, and a final agent stitches them into a deal packet.
Sample Python Skeleton for Chaining and Orchestration
python
CopyEdit
from langchain.vectorstores import Chroma
from langchain.llms import Ollama
from langchain.chains import SequentialChain
# 1. Chunk and index all docs
# (Assume you've already chunked and indexed)
# 2. Retrieval
def get_relevant_chunks(query, vector_db):
    return vector_db.similarity_search(query, k=5)
# 3. Orchestrate agents
def run_agents(query, agents, vector_db):
    context_chunks = get_relevant_chunks(query, vector_db)
    context = " ".join([c.page_content for c in context_chunks])
    input_text = f"{query}\nContext:\n{context}"
    outputs = []
    for agent in agents:
        response = agent(input_text)
        outputs.append(response)
        input_text = f"{response}\n{context}"  # carry forward output as new context
    return outputs
# 4. Example agent functions (pointing to different models)
def agent1(input_text):
    # call local LLM via Ollama, etc.
    return ollama_model_1.generate(input_text)
def agent2(input_text):
    # another model or another LoRA/adapter
    return ollama_model_2.generate(input_text)
# 5. Chain together
agents = [agent1, agent2]
final_outputs = run_agents("Draft Chapter 1", agents, vector_db)
Key Takeaway
You can’t escape context limits, but you can chunk, chain, route, merge, and summarize to build bigger workflows.
No one LLM, even at 128GB RAM, can “see” a whole 300k word book at once. But a distributed agent system (like ApolloGPT) can handle massive projects by breaking them up.
You must design around these limitations: the system is about architecture and smart workflows, not just raw model size.
